I really don't get the deal with you retired guys. You quit EQ, but still post on the "EQ" shaman boards. That makes as much sense as dropping out of highschool and hanging out in the school parking lot. As far as the "almost 5k wizard" - wouldn't that be the "almost 5k Time-flagged wizard"?

/rant off

Everquest is a heavy time-sink game, it is part of the rpg game genre that are known to be time-sink games. There are two types of people in this world: Process people and Product people. Process people enjoy the path to a solution and tend to move slower than others "to enjoy the scenery". Product people are the opposite in that they are only interested in the end result, and will move quickly to achieve that result.
Amazingly enough, Everquest caters to both groups of people- group content for the Progress people and raid content for the Product people.
I think you are a "Product" person and the route that EQ gave you was unachievable by time constraints, undesirable game mechanics, or general hate for other people...ummm.. pick one. Hence, because of this you became discontent/angry/raving mad....ummm... pick one again. The quickest way to become bored with EQ is to run out of things to do- ask the high-end folks. High end guilds have a heavy turnover before and during new expansions, because they run out of things to do. My "time-geared" monk friend has quit after "Kunark/Velious/Luclin/PoP/RZ/Elemental/Time and is thinking about quitting again before OoW comes out. He runs out of things to do, and therefore becomes bored. Had you joined a high-end guild to compete with the "almost 5k wizard" you would probably still be quitting due to "My friends and I ran out of stuff to do at L65 and we decided not to throw more good money after bad once what content we could access was used up." Basically, Everquest doesn't move fast enough to keep you occupied. That being said....here is some food for thought.

PoP was tailored to the 46+ crowd, and it accomplished two things: It got the high-end guilds out of Luclin to make room for guilds below them to progress, and it split up raid content in an effort to split up the bottleneck that Luclin raid farming zones (Ssra/VT) had created. Instead of 50 players each having to individually camp mutiple mobs to have the ability to raid with their guilds- they chose to go with a flag system where raiding would key them for further raid content. The only other option is to completely remove the flag/key system. Sounds like a great idea right? not quite... and here is why:

Problem: Casual players complain about group trials to access PoV/PoS and key farming for CoD, BoT, HoH.

Solution: by Devs is to unlock those said zones with specific levels (i.e. 55, 62)

Solution results: Casuals move first to the zone with the best drops, after they have collected up what they want- they move to the zone with the best xp. (i.e. BoT which for accessible zones offered the best drops/xp)

New Problem: Casuals are now bottlenecked in BoT (Tactics if they did the PoS key quest)

New Solution: Devs created LDoN with 4 zones of equal content, new drops, and point purchased items.

Solution Results: Casual Players move to LDoN to collect up the drops, some pick up purchased items. Casual players get bored with "un-interesting dungeons" (how is this less interesting than BoT?)

Casuals Proposed Solution: Unlock Sol Ro, lower the cost of LDoN point purchased gear, have equal lines of gear in all camps.

Pros/Cons

Unlock Sol Ro- heres the problem with this idea

1. No-Drop gear off mini-named is sub-par to LDoN purchased items / Elemental Tradeskill items....gratz rot.
2. Ornate drop rate is sporadic, you may see three pieces drop in 1 hour or you may see 1 piece drop in 4 hours. Guess what drops the most- the same pieces that are the cheapest in the bazaar (i.e. BoT commonly dropped ornate.)
3. No easily accessible zone-out. I still see melees that port in and forgot to buy gate potions. These guys already had a heads-up and forgot. How many /ooc's you think there are going to be if Sol Ro is opened up to the masses? How many people are going to train the zone-in of Sol Ro in attempts to find that zone-out?

Lower the cost of LDoN purchased items- anyone heard of Black Mire?

1. Black Mire gear costs 760 points in Guk, and is "slightly" inferior to Incorporeal Chain which goes for 1492 points in EF. Stat-wise it is still better than Ornate minus the focus effects. Black Mire is also designed to wear "upon purchase". Yes, you can burn points to augment it, but for may- just the gear itself is an upgrade. It takes 15 guk wins to unlock the Black Mire, but once its unlocked it just takes 15 "normal" wins anywhere to purchase another piece of Black Mire. On my server the avg success rate of a normal LDoN win runs around 80-90% and the total time for setup-completion is about 1 hour- 1 1/4 hours. For those that play less it will take a while, but you are "guaranteed" the item. There is no named camping, no bad rolls, no kill-stealing- you have the item "in the bag". For many classes that cannot solo well, LDoN is a godsend- they get nice gear without having to farm plat.

Equal Lines of gear at each camp:

1. Great idea, it would kill the " I will only group if we go to X adv camp". Putting equal lines of gear will allow casuals to work on new gear "right out of the gate."

This post was long and lengthy, but that point I am trying to get at is that the "majority" of the populous will go "risk/reward in mind" to where the gear drops and then to where the xp is the best. It doesn't matter if the zone is called "BoT" or "PoTime". If you lock people out of certain zones they complain that are being targeted because of a specific play-style. Make the zone open and they complain they are being bottlenecked. Revamping old zones does not resolve this issue either unless they are better than what is currently available. (Ex: Droga/Nurga was revamped with Droga giving comparable xp to BoT along with "in many cases" better loot availability, but the zone is mostly empty due to being "a non-PoK portal" zone. Ferubi gives xp comparable to Elementals with comparable gear, but its a "non-PoK portal zone" so it is largely empty. Do you think LDoN would have been accessible as it was without 3 of the 5 zones being accessible by the PoK books? You can give a herd of cattle 200,000 acres of land to roam on, but in the end they will only hang around the 1-2 acres that has the waterhole.

Shaman B is in a smattering of ornate (all looted), some lower end raid gear from Luclin/Velious and has a few choice items from Tier3 raids. She has all her spells and a few AA, mostly up to Cann 5. Shes a hard core raider wannabe, and is essentially pampered by her guild, being lead around by the nose, gets summoned a lot and in general is average at best.
IF any of us were on an alt, we would want shaman A in our group....all things equal we would pick someone with talent and character over gear any day...thing is all things arent equal.

On top of that you want them to pay an additional $5 to be bottlenecked in one raid zone? There is a strong indy raid culture on my server, and partaking in the culture can get you Elemental flagged. Whether casual or hardcore, I would say the majority of the populous partakes in these pickup raids to get flagged for better xp/ better drops, and the amount of time spent doing this is equal to about the time taken to do 1-2 LDoNs. We have another player on our server who runs open VT runs on the weekend, and only asks for "good attendance rate". We have another player on the server that runs open Tormax,Vindi,AoW raids. I would say a large majority of my server does raid in one shape or form for whatever reason they see fit.
When PoP came out, it was tailored to the 46+ crowd. I could count the number of lvl 46 players in those zones on one hand even if I had all my fingers cut off- it was 0. There, quite literally, was no gear at that point in the game that could have prepared a lvl 46 toon for the beating they were about to get in any of the Tier 1 zones. Many players didn't even mess with the Planes until they were 60+, many chose to take the easier route and kill light blues in Velks until 62.
GoD had a revamp, and many of the xp zones (not trials) were reset on dmg dealt and total mob hitpoints. The changes are very noticable to include Natimbi / Qinimi being nice zones for <65 and Barindu / Ferubi being nice zones for the 65 crowd. The 8 mob types to chose one is purely a cosmetic complaint- its unimaginative, but in all honesty if the mob gives you xp it can be a giant rat for all I care. Loot table distribution is still horrid, but I think the GoD raiders are hit harder by this than the casuals. Killing a raid named to only see 1 piece of gear and 3 berserker tomes drop doesn't impress anyone. All in all, GoD was a lousy expansion for everyone, partly in fact that it wasn't finished and still is broken in places. GoD wasn't an attack on the casual player as all players got the "WTH?" reaction upon "zoning in", so pointing a finger at the raiders blaming them is pointless- it was SOE's foul-up. The last two expansions before GoD were catered to the casual players if you remember -LDoN and LoY.

Going to use an example on this one- Cable Television companies. Many people enjoy Cable television, but the majority watches 4-5 channels. The Cable Companies would lose money if they were to charge for each individual channel, because lets be honest- 4/5 of the channels you get are fluff. That being said, many people would only order 4-5 channels, but over time, would narrow their channel selection to 2-3 to "save money". Bringing this example back to EQ so it makes some sense....
The avg cost for a new EQ expansion seems to run around $15-25, but let's say they split it up to where the expansion costs $10-20, but the raid add-on costs $5. Over time, the populous will see the avg cost as being $10-20 and having to pay $5 to get the raid content. With charging a set price for everything you will have people wondering "did I really need all of this?", but with splitting up the price you will have "I have to pay $5 more to prolong the life of a game I play?" Internet service providers used to run off the "pay for what you play" system, but eventually moved to the monthly rate. The "pay for what you play" worked for those that "played" very little, but was hindering to those that enjoyed the internet, but didn't want to have to watch the clock. The point I am trying to make is that splitting up the cost of an expansion will go from "I save $5 from not paying for content I will not use" to "I have to pay $5 more to enjoy the raid content." In the end, it will be all about perspective with how it looks being more important than how it is.

-Alan

This is a slap in the face! And all those who agree with this statement need to take a moment to truly realize the impact of what he's saying. Caster's spells are *cough* 'Balanced' against Melee's damage. A melee gives two S***s about 66-70 spells. The melee will get their damage table raised, their additional attack, and most likely their /disc books instantly upon gaining levels. Tanks will get their additional hit points, etc. What does the wizard get? A handful of hitpoints and the inability to reach any of the spells they paid good money to have access to. Effectively making a level 70 Wizard = 65 Wizard + 10 hps.

The same is true for the shaman. We depend on our spells just as much as the wizard and other pure casters. Alan comparing Caster spells to Melee Equipment is shortsighted.

And to those who say that not everyone needs all their spells, that's not what we're saying (/ack I'm saying). I have a few empty slots in my spellbook. What I'm saying is that the vast majority of the spells should be available without a /raid force. The ancient line is not in my grasp. I'm aware of that. But the upgrades to buffs, heals, canni, etc [our core 6 spells] should be 1 group attainable or quested. It is a total lack of imagination on Sony's part to just cram these spells on the biggest, baddest mobs available to keep them from the general EQ population. I beleive in No Drop, just not Never Drop.

Predicus
Mobs that are too hard for the players are set paths too, and so are flags, and paths to complete epics, but they get changed. Noone said anything about making it easier, just providing more options. Options that don't require 5 and 6 hour raids, that most people don't have time for. And why is it whining because people want a chance? Noone is whining to you, this is a discussion.

Its called content-hopping, and it is especially noticable with the casual community. Like someone said earlier, new content is designed from the top down and there is a reason for that. SOE is "trying" to design content that is challenging to ALL players who partake in the game- your pure casual to your die-hard raider. This is obviously in their best interest because it nets them the most profit from the game. What SOE still doesn't understand is that players (especially those that do not complete old content) will try to jump old content as a way to play catch up. Its an assumption by SOE that everyone will progress in a linear fashion and "wait their turn in line". Unfortunately, that never works out right because everyone wants the newest and greatest or at least close to it. Take Vex Thal/PoP for example. Those guilds who progressed through Ssra/VT were able to make a smooth progression through PoP up to the Elementals and on through Time. Some instances were tricky and difficult, but in most cases these guilds were successful. What came later were guilds who decided they wanted to raid PoP, but didn't want to go through the trouble to farm VT. These guilds hit a few "brickwalls", and had to do a bit more of Lower PoP farming to achieve the same goals as those that went through VT. The casual player doesn't follow the straight line in progression that developers think they will. They may or may not grind to 65, they may or may not work on aa's, they may or may not raid, they may or may not play the game when something new comes out. The casual player in general is very fickle, and probably gets a better view of the game because of this. These are the guys that know that X mob in Y zone drops a nifty item because they got bored one day and went to check the zone out. These are the guys that have been 58 for 6 months because " I didn't feel like xp-ing today, so I made an alt." These are the guys that are posting about odd quest items that they found off of X mob in Y zone. Some casuals limit themselves to 1-3 zones, and I can see why they get bored. I don't because I know that is the quickest way to burn myself out.
What I do is I may xp in Sol Ro for a week, then I may do some LDoNs for a week, then I may tradeskill/work on quests for a week, and then I may work on my alts for a week. It keeps me busy and I don't get burned out, and it creates an in-game break that makes each facet of the game fresh when I come back to it. Two things I am currently working on now is scouring EQ with my monk buddy trying to see how many right-clickies we can collect up; I am also attempting to see how insanely I can twink out an alt without having to level him up to 65. It works for me and keeps the game fresh.
The replay value of EQ is incredibly high- for crying out loud we have people that have been playing for 5 years and are still playing! Raiding is one aspect of the game, just like xp grinding, bazaar trading, and camp farming. Each aspect of the game has their pros and cons, and none of them are entirely necessary to play the game. If you chose not to partake in an aspect you need to except the good with the bad. If you don't want to grind, don't complain about others passing you up in levels- so on and so forth. BoT, LDoN, Sol Ro, Natimbi, Barindu, Ferubi, and the Sewer trials do not require Time gear, so if any of these zones are your normal hunting grounds- then don't worry about how many hitpoints the Time Geared guy has. His normal hunting ground is PoTime- where those hitpoints and resists are needed.
Most raids never hit the 5th-6th hour, raiders in general can't stay focused long enough. The only time you really see that is with new content that hasn't been cracked yet. Your avg high end raider is putting in about 16-20 hours per week. Raids usually run for 3-4 hours, sometimes less depending on whats up. Many raiders log in for the raid, and when the raid is over- they log out. These same players have other things that go on in their life, just like casual players- they just happen to be able to find a block of time that they can play in. They also typically don't waste time forming up either like you see with pick-up raids. Raid force is told to be at Y location at X time- within 20-30 mins the raid is set up and final afk's are taken, and the raid is moving. They hit what targets they have that are up, and start wrapping up the raid about 2 1/2-3 hours later. People make assumptions that raiding takes 5-6 hour chunks of time and you have to be on 7 days a week, but that is simply not the case- no one wants to do that. There is simply not enough time in the day. SOE knows people can't play in 6 hour stretches, so they don't tailor the raid content to take 6 hours to complete. Being a high-end raider does not require 40-50 hours of dedication a week to the game, and I think a lot of people make that assumption.

If you don't want to raid but still want to progress, EverQuest is not your game. The sooner people accept that, the sooner people will be happy or at least, understand how the system works.

There are current and future games that offer more than one way to play the game to max your character, but EverQuest is not one of them. SOE's design philosophy isn't just "top down" (to borrow Predicus' phrase), it's "raid down"- they design raid content that may or may not be applicable to non-raid players. The best part is they advertise it as "character levels 50+" and technically they're right- if a L50 player grinds to 65, joins a raid guild and then gets flagged for all the zones in the expansion, yes, this expansion's content is for them.

Makes eminently perfect sense.

There should be gear attainable by a single group of players that is the exact same as the best gear coming out of time. Maybe it doesnt have the same cool look. Or the neat name. Or the particle effects, but there should be a raid path and a Group path to all the content.

the logic of this is baffling. if there were equal rewards for killing something with 6 people versus 60, then no one would EVER do it for raiding. part of the difficulty of raiding is coordinating large groups of people because they are NECESSARY to kill things. while this may not be great, ie cleric/ench/war demand for certain encounters (rathe, vallon zek, innoruuk, etc.), it is how the game is setup. if you were to actually make darkblade of the warlord 1 groupable, every single 65 warrior in the game would have it by now and all the casuals would be just as bored as the rest.

bottom line is, raiding is MORE difficult than 1 grouping things. it requires good leadership, strong organization, good internet connections, and lots of playtime. this is the natural way that seperates players. just because i have time gear doesn't mean i won't group with friends that do not, or that are in smaller or not even in guilds. if they were actually stupid enough to make all the best stuff 1 groupable we'd have diablo2 on our hands. simply, raiding IS harder than grouping, and hence it reaps better rewards, and by thinking that you deserve 100% equal rewards just because your playstyle doesn't fit sony's notion of "difficulty" you are sorely mistaken. lets face it, their notion of difficulty hasn't been all that creative, either.

while LDON was not an expansion i particularly liked, it did do some good things for once. YES, there should be decent stuff obtainable with 1 grp (or less) with invested time, skill, questing, etc. should it be equal to raiding gear? i agree. but 100% equality is perhaps the single most idiotic argument imaginable. 1 group content is easier than raiding. it always has, and i don't see that changing any time soon. to be able to gather 5 other people who can devote some time to working on stuff is infinately less difficult than it is to run, manage, maintain, and control a group of 75+ people who all have different playtimes, preferences, personalities, etc. while i agree there needs to be more balance, ie quested or ldon type stuff with more appropriate rewards of the times, if you simply made all items in the game doable with a single group you have diablo2 which many have played and many got bored of within weeks.

as for spells, yea i agree there should be lots of ways to obtain your spells. but certainly not all of them. some things just need to be for the few so everyone else has something to drool over and work towards. making crucial spells for the elite is probably a bad idea. defining classes by their crucial spells and hence by their guild status is obviously not smart. but, that being said, a shaman with time's antithesis slows a mob by 75% just as does any shaman without it. in general EQ has been decent about spells (most ancients being not THAT crucial) and their availability. you can bazaar equip any caster class with the vast majority of their spells, and just because your a wizard and do 6500 crits instead of 7500 crits from time geared/focus'd wizards or whatever with your spells doesn't make you worthless. in fact i'd take a skilled player over a better equipped one (of all but the crucial tank/clr/ench, a whole nother issue there) in most situations. i've done fire tables with time equipped people that are horrible and don't know how to quad and had much better grps with bazaar equipped people that know *** they're doing. while equipment > much, especially for the key classes, it still can't make up for skill in a lot of cases. moreso than many people realize i think.

everyone is NOT entitled to the same gear/spells. you ARE entitled to the same opportunity to achieve/earn them. If you do not want to do what it takes to get them, that is your option. your 12 bucks does not mean you should be able to automatically get everything everyone else has.

finally, some sense. joril's post is quite correct. and i agree with your two choices. there is simply just no easy way to balance raiders vs. non raiders.

one is break down and join a rading guild. there are those that dont require 5 hours a night and 5 days a week (though some do). I know one guild on karana that doesnt require that kind of attendance and they are knocking on times door. they are allied with 3 or 4 other guilds.

something you may all want to look at. some guildmates of mine started this group of casual raiders on our server. they have a few set rules, but not many, and are not exclusive AT ALL in who they allow to raid, barring a few very rare cases. they flag anyone regardless of guild affiliation, level, personality, etc. (assuming you don't cause a problem to the raid leaders). so, for example, on my server, you do NOT have to "be a raider" to get elemental flagged. or even be in a raiding guild. just get your flags once, like everyone else had/has to do, and don't raid again, its like the other timesinks from expansions, you do it once, then never look back. level 70 will certainly make the ease of elemental access more accessible as well. and there's a whole lot of nice 1 groupable things in the elemental planes, and tradeskill drops, and etc.etc.

What would be nice is dynamic raiding. Ldon gave us a somewhat dynamic option with single groups, but would be nice to have the option of scaling to raid size. Give that 20 person raid a substantially similar experience that a 70 person raid sees. Obviously, the larger raid can handle tougher/more mobs with corresponding more loot, but an option would be nice.
Scale raids to size/levels and us casual players have a wider option to experience the same content as the hardcore folks. Most casual players can handle a couple 3-4 hour raids per week.

i agree. i'm not sure how hard it would be to impliment, but being able to scale the difficulty of the raid due to the size might solve some issues with clr/ench/war #'s, etc.

all and all, i'm not offended or shocked like i'm amazed some of you are about this. i recently switched mains from shaman to my shadowknight, and i'm not up in arms about him never recieving his ancient spell from ToV or VT (if there were one). he doesn't have all his spells, including the qvic ancient bite of chaos (quite a nice spell), nor should he, but i'm not FREAKING OUT BECAUSE I WONT EVER GET IT. i'm not useless because wizards have more hitpoints than me as a tank. the game is still about fun. if you're not having fun because you won't get EVERY ITEM AND SPELL OMGOMG then perhaps you need to think about what is fun for you about the game. for me, doing avatar of war with 10, or soloing zlandicar, or doing the skyiron cudgel of the ancients/greenmist quests were fun. much about EQ is the work you put into it and the rewards you earn yourself. for me, that is the fun, and recieving the items/spells/gear/keys is all just part of the process. sure they need to better balance the ldon type stuff with the increases in GoD gear, so perhaps rather than constantly complaining about how raiders are handed free loot and make you feel inadequate because they play 50 hours a week and you play 40 and tradeskill, key farm, adventure, etc., you should instead focus on trying to encourage the devs to have more ldon/god trials type content for a single group with decent rewards/exp for the time invested. that being said, it is my opinion, though, that these rewards should never be completely equal to raid gear, otherwise there would be little point in raiding and the game wouldn't hold anyone's attention.

BlargX
I think you're looking very hard for things to be sick about? You certainly sound more than a little hysterical to me. Take a chill pill, son. If you always overreact when people disagree with you, you're going to have a very hard life.

To Drinnar:

"You simply cannot make single group encounters that will require the same amount of time/effort as a raid."

Tremendously untrue. Many people can get into top raiding guilds within weeks because they know someone who makes it a smooth slide for them all the way up. I've known too many dozens to count.

The guy the raid is hard for is the first guy doing it and his buncha people figuring things out. How many of us are that first guy? Virtually none. In the entire history of the game, very few of us have ever been the first guy to figure anything out -- there are too many things to figure, and most of them have been gotten to first, by somebody else.

Raiding can be extremely easy; so much so that people AFK while raiding is very common even in the top guilds. Being able to follow for the most part often means little more than not being too lousy at your job. That...is not exactly a challenge. At least for a good proportion of us.

Compare that to grouping or doing a small raid. If you screw up, it counts, because there aren't endless replacements for you waiting. When I raid, if one of our six shamans doesn't get slow in, the other will. If your slow doesn't land in a group, you can be screwed. Same with getting aggro, healing, crowd control -- everything.

Raids can be and often are chock full of screw ups and dolts who barely know their jobs. It doesn't take much skill to hit 65 these days, or even get most flags. Being a raider is next to no indication of your skill these days or of the work you have put into your character or intend to.

Please, this idea that raiders do so much work -- it's way too sloppy and categorical to say very freely at all. Most raiders are followers and it's quite easy for most classes to get quite a fair amount of slacking in on raids.

Does raiding sort out the spazzes and the jerks any better than grouping does, just by its nature? Not necessarily. If you screw up your job in a group or are definitely sub-par, you're outta there. You can do that for years as a raider.

Now, so much for the amount of work and skill a raider does and has, one half of the holy equation that more work and skill should equal the other half -- that is, exponentially better gear and access to 90% of an expansion's fighting areas instead of 5%, with another 5% left over for banks and friendly bazaars and such.

Everybody I know who has joined a time guild has gotten tons of gear extremely quickly. It's like they busted a piggy bank and the goodies just came rolling out. Within weeks, or less, people who had been fairly new to the game and crying about their lack of elemental gear suddenly were completely stuffed with easily gotten time gear.

The amount of hours put in, the ingenuity showed, the skill level required to get that gear are a fraction of what other people in the game must do to get gear nowhere near that level, if they simply don't have the flags and guild alliances. Skill level? Please. I know these guys I'm talking about, raid with them myself regularly, group with them, and few of them were exceptional in any way; they just had a good thing going for them when they got hooked up. And god or whatever bless 'em for that -- somebody has to have a good thing going somewhere. But to use their combination of good fortune and their ability to spend long contiguous blocks of time on a very regular basis to then posit them as somehow virtuous or hard working or very much more accomplished than others and exceptionally deserving of dramatically outsized rewards merely because someone got them some quick flags as soon as they joined up is just not indicative of a terribly balanced game, or viewpoint on the game.

Much like the level 46 shaman in Plane of Earth, the guy who has been playing the game for three or four or five months and is in the Plane of Time is not the game's hero and a living showpiece of the game's good design, but shows up the poor design of the game and how much it can be tweaked with to essentially bypass any semblance of balance.

Not that even the most boneheaded raider will usually admit how easy he has had it -- that would be like finding a wealthy 18 year old frat kid who has never had a job who doesn't insist he's a self-made man and a genius for keeping a B average.

Please, let's all of us heroes out here have a little humility. Very few of us are the daring adventurers we would like to claim to be when comparing ourselves to the casual players. I'd bet solid money that most casual players have explored a lot more of the EQ world than most raiders, at least those of the last couple of years. Adventurers? Hard-working? Daring?

Most raiders I know these days zipped through their levels as quickly as possible with mudflation gear earned or given to them very early on, zipped into a raid guild as soon as possible with very underdeveloped skills, and spend their time there following somebody else's directions to do things that have been laid out for them and already accomplished dozens of times by their guilds and thousands of times across the servers by the time they ever get to the battles themselves. Then they spend their time split between raiding, LDON, and the plane of earth on the easiest mobs they can find, and maybe at the Plane of Fire tables if they are casters.

That's IT. Adventuring? Exploring? Blazing new trails? Refining high level skills? Puh-lease.

The myth of raiders being supermen and somehow better, smarter, braver, more knowledgeable or whatever than the average player is one of the more ridiculous ones EQ players have ever been silly enough to grant their overbloated egos. Like a tall tale, it gets more absurd with every retelling.

Which is all fine, as long as you really remember it's all just a story.

It is the enforced mentality of pop that justifies for some that raid items should in cases be better than that earned by a group, in light of the necessary work and time needed to gain those items. However, that is one of the flaws of pop. Backflagging does not need to be a necessary part of raiding.

Perhaps the argument should be altered slightly, in that people should not have to join a guild of 70 to get the best items. Imagine an instanced raid area, that alike Ldon where it uses the peoples levels to create an appropiate dungeon, a raid area that automates according to how many people you use. The content would be harder but would drop more of x item. So a 70 man raid, mob y would drop many x items, compared to a 20 man raid where mob y would drop less items. I guess this example, could be extended to cater for a group of 6, however, the chance of the x item dropping is considerably reduced.

Thats just my view and ideas though.

much of this is a moot point, also. OoW and level 70 will trivialize PoP just like PoP and level 65 did to luclin content. the reason you don't hear everyone bitching about BEING FORCED TO RAID LUCLIN CONTENT OMGOMG is because 65 made it trivial, so it became doable with very small grps of people.

for you non raiding ppl, simply think of PoP flags as keying. if you don't like to raid, fine, raid to get your zone access and then stop. everyone did vp/vt keys. no one liked doing them (in general), and the people that hated questing did them as fast as possible and never did them again. they realized that keying(flagging) was a necessity in the game to seperate those who have put the time in and those that haven't. its the exact same with PoP. plenty of pickup raids that can get u flagged to plenty of decent zones, like i already said on my server there is a completely open pickup group that is all the way to elementals, and this will only get more powerful after OoW's release. you don't see me here bitching constantly about how i hate doing the first 1-6 GoD trials because they are single group content. i prefer raid content, but i'm about to start bitching about them. i put the time in, got them done, and moved on. eq is about the challenge (or in key cases very often, time) that you put into it, and the rewards you gain afterwards. like said before if you want to be handed everything go play diablo2, and burn out 10x faster than you ever would in eq.

I understand the rationale you guys may have behind saying that non-raiders shouldn't get the same level of gear and spells, etc., that raiders get. It's just wrong though.

Putting all the histrionics aside, this is a game. People play for the same reasons -- to have a kickass character. I don't think they should be punished because they haven't played the game a certain way.

the simple reason that i say raid gear should always be > non raid gear in some form is the simple logistics of it. for the current setup of everquest at the moment, to do this would make raiding pointless and turn the game into a diablo2 like environment.

also i think its important to emphasize just how rare these occurances are. the average raider is/does put a lot more time into playing than the average casual, especially in large bursts or on a nightly basis. go ahead and flame but IN GENERAL this is the case, i don't care about any random examples as there obviously are plenty of them. this game is more about time than anything else, zone access is 1 thing, but the longer you play the better you're going to get. raid guild players, running out of things to do solo/grp, join guilds to raid, and in general have higher playtime than the average eq people. these people burn out faster than most, so the high end game is designed to try to keep them interested and give them something to do (OoW).

i have no problems with people that play the game casually. as a college student i am able to give lots of spare time (more than i should heh) to this game. what i do have a problem with is this mentality of entitlement that people have gotten. just because i pay the cover at a bar doesn't mean that i'm entitled to any/all free drinks that i want. just because you buy an expansion doesn't mean you're instantly given some right to explore all of its content on your own terms. if that were the case go play D and D where YOU control the content. and lets be realistic as well, here, most people that play a lot, even in non raiding type guilds, do get to experience/have gotten to experience most of the previous content due to guilds progressing, moving on, etc. to whine about PoP as blocking people is really quite pointless as level 70 is going to completely trivialize every encounter from hedge to quarm, just like PoP trivialized all luclin content. if you want to see all the content now, follow the path and raid. if you don't, be patient and wait till the content becomes trivial enough to do with just a grp or a small affiliation of people (can't be a raid omg). i'm all for them making ldon type attainable gear better, but complete equality, while a decent notion in itself, is not viable at all for the game. it would unbalance everything, and its pretty hard to argue with the unparalelled success the game has had over the last 5+ years.

Just as was said. Some spells are like TA. Unless you raid and put that much more effort into it you wont get them.

Comparing Fo7, Malos, Quiessence, Blood of Saryrn, etc to TA is ridiculous.

The only reason I would ever want Fo7 or Malos in my book is for convenience. Torpor and Quiessence are likewise nice to have, but by no means geared solely for high-end raiders. Blood of Saryrn? All I hear on the boards is people bitching about how DoTs are useless outside of soloing. These are not spells that drop from the darkest depths of PoTime B. If there was Quarm-dropped shaman spell that was a 3-tick DoT for double BoS's total damage, I'd drool over it, then forget about it, because I'm not heading to PoTime anytime soon.

The problem is, the spells that will end up costing me 50k+ a piece are either convenience items or standard upgrades to existing spells. There's nothing so remarkable about them that warrants their drops being isolated to high-end content.

Fo7? It's a group FoSoul. I bought FoS for less than 1k. Why should I pay 49k more for the group version? Convenience? not likely. I've got canni IV and soon canni V. My group will deal with a short break for rebuffs.

Malos? It's a nice, but relatively minor upgrade to Malo. Not worth 20k simply because it drops in Tier 4 PoP.

Quiessence? Nice upgrade over Torpor, but I don't hear anything about Quiessence that makes me think it's anything but a nice spell for xp grouping or soloing.

BoS? People don't even cast these in groups because fights are over so fast. It's a spell for soloers. Why should it be restricted to a raid drop?

TA, elemental armor, etc are great rewards for the raiders. I don't have any problem with rewards for hard work. Should there be spells that are similarly geared? Sure. Make them very powerful and make them no drop.

The spells I want, Fo7, Malos, Quiessence, BoS, are standard upgrades, not game breakers, for the non-raider.
Predicus
First, before you get all pissy, look at my magelo.

Second, I already said this isn't going to happen in EQ. I don't expect people who don't have the mental capacity to think outside of the current RPG paradigm to be able to imagine a world where raiding isn't considered "work" or somehow a more noble way to play the game.

Let's drop the words "raid content" and "group content". I realize this isn't going to change your mind because you think raiding should be the end-all, be-all of every RPG everywhere, but I'm going to go through it anyway.

The size of the force that is required to bring a target down should determine the types of challenges that the force will have to overcome (i.e. variety of different spells that go off to reflect the probable wider variety of classes present to combat that, number of additional critters to face, etc) and the volume of loot.

There's no reason to make one piece of content that simply requires larger numbers drop higher quality gear, or spells. This isn't coming from some newb who feels entitled to every piece of gear etc. Future games must design with that in mind. Character progression should not depend on who you play the game with.

I understand what you are saying. I enjoyed Velious immensely and one thing that I did like was the large loot tables that raid mobs had. Killing boss mobs like King Tormax or AoW would net a guild 3-4 items per kill. PoP mobs typically give us 2 items per kill, and I think this was to slow down progression so people wouldn't rush through new content as quickly as they have in the past. Right now the game runs off a numbers system where the amount of people needed will determine the quality of loot dropped. As raid events become trivial the items they give also become trivial. I just recently finished up the Prayer Shawl quest, when it came out I believe it required about 3 groups to complete the shawl crawl. I did the shawl crawl with 3 friends, and it was still trivial. I seriously think I could have accomplished the crawl with one melee class, but at the time I didn't want to screw up the event. The shawl while no longer the greatest item is still used by many until they get to PoTime and find something better. That being sad, if this event is challenging to complete by two people should it net Elemental / Time quality loot? I don't think so. SOE seems to run off of some calculation of time/content/group force to determine drop quality- sometimes they hit the mark and sometimes they are off. I still find hard LDoN / Tipt / Vxed to be challenging and I think the drops from those events are in-line with the challenge. The only drawback to this is that Tipt / Vxed still require a "specific" group with many classes being left out.
Here's where I see the problem with groupable / raidable content:

1. Players will take the easiest approach to completing a goal.
(Ex: using a chanter pet for Tipt / Vxed trials)
Problem: with this strategy you now need the pet babysitting crew
further locking in a "set group".
(i.e debuffer, snarer, buffers, and the chanter)

2. Players will jump content.
(Ex: Players that zerg to 65 and then app to Time+ guilds)
Problem: Usually these jumps in content are during a guild's "farming"
period when they are reaping their rewards for previous encounters.
The loss of player skill/gear will, for the most part, go unnoticable
due to the "triviality" of present content. It may have been
challenging the first few times, but after a period of equipping
the guild the content becomes trivial.

3. Players will go with the best time/risk/reward.
(Ex: Raiders will hit hard targets once or twice to flag them to a
farming point.)
Problem: How many guilds have you seen that report "We are now
farming Coirnav!" It's a high risk encounter and therefore unless
people need the flag most don't want to mess with the event.
Snoww had a great idea in putting equal gear in the game for groups,
but with long timesinks. Those that chose to progress as a group
would take longer, but would not hit the ceiling as quickly. The only
problem with this is that raiding is still superior in that it will
accomplish the same goals in better time. If there isn't the time-sink
to group content then, whether people enjoy raiding or not, there is
no reason to raid.

At this point in the game, every expansion becomes a fight between the raiders and the groupers. Arguments over groupable content rewards and raiding content rewards is commonplace, and I think the developers are finding it harder now than ever to appease everyone. The raiders wanted more raidable content to help offset the bottlenecks that are commonplace in their environment while the groupers want the gap between time/gear to be shorten or completely done away with.
EQ is quickly becoming two seperate games with two seperate environments. Some guilds want smaller raids so they can have content to experience while other guilds don't because they would have to split up their playerbase. Casual players want better and quicker progression rates while hardcore players do not want their achievements to be trivialized quicker. I highly doubt any of these issues will be resolved in EQ. It looks like SOE is trying to resolve them in EQ2, but it looks like it will severely handicap playstyles to accomplish this. Things like Trivial Loot Code kills farming, kiting nerfs will kill the soloing power of certain classes, so the best solo'ers will be the endurance/burst dps classes. Less items in the market plus the add in of equipment breaking creates a higher priced market. If raiding is removed or nerfed it takes away the current end-game content, so they will have to replace it with something that will occupy/interest long term customers. Further splitting off the classes like what they are reporting in EQ2 will create more issues when the game progresses in that all classes will be handicapped as to what they can get by their counterpart. This will create less innovative aspects to classes and further the "mudflation" of abilities that we see today.

"Disregarding the enormous effort that went into reaching the zones the raiding guilds play in cheapens your argument."

Actually, veeshanmurtak, you've cheapened your own argument by not making one. You've merely reinstated what is already known instead of making a new point or even bothering to address what you purport to be responding to. And yes, you have indeed gotten into the argument with your post.

I never said no raiders and nobody in a guild ever worked. I think the argument that some people have, that guild members or raiders in general have put in prodigious amounts of work, generally done with devotion and a high level of skill, and sometimes lots of ingenuity to boot, is the standing argument at this time when people talk about raiders getting huge rewards compared to non-raiders. This is indeed why they say that level of reward is deserved.

I was pointing out other sides too because THAT SIDE is ALREADY emphasized and reiterated so much it's taken as though it were common sense and not merely just an idea on very shaky ground. It's well-defended and understood, and what I was saying is that it is highly exaggerated more often than not. Your calling me to task for it is ridiculous. The obvious needs no belaboring; my post was made in part because of the relentless belaboring the obvious already gets, and how much it is celebrated and pointed to, to the virtual exclusion of what is probably the reality for most people's experience in the game.

It's more than safe to say most people in a guild did not figure out how to do every encounter themselves. It's more than safe to say that it can be incredibly easy to scoop up great gear when you join a raid gear even off encounters you've never heard of before, much less mastered your part in, much less developed the strategies to beat.

The endless patting on the back that raiders give themselves is very undeserved in most cases. I say this as someone who has been a raider for most of his EQ career, going back five years.

Really, being Rogue_07 or Shaman_05 or Cleric_18 doesn't make anyone a dynamic adventurer, skilled player, or anything else.

And though many of us would likely hate to acknowledge it, most of us are NOT the exception.

And many raids succeed not because of great playing and high skill levels -- but despite poor playing, numerous AFK's rolling through the classes, strategic and tactical bungling...just because it can sometimes be almost as easy to succeed as fail.

The flipside to this is that quite a few casual players are indeed highly skilled. And their frequent tendency to NOT just try to get through and pretty much ignore virtually the entire game in their race to become high-level raiders often means they've put in vastly more adventuring, exploring, and figuring out ways to tackle mobs and situations than a great many raiders have.

The equation of raider = better player is just not a one that should be taken at face value as glibly and fervently as it always is. Of course, who doesn't like to flatter themselves? Flatter the living heck out of themselves? So raiders will usually pump up the volume big time on how special they are.

They're not. They're regular players, just like anyone else.

And getting their goodies is not at all a sign of how hard they worked or anything else positive. Get in the right guild and stuff can be pretty much handed to you, from flags to loot to tradeskill materials.

The guy running around in elemental gear or time gear or whatever is not necessarily any better or harder working a player than anyone else. Quite the contrary, quite often.

BlargX
"Why raid?" is the refrain I always hear from raiders. Why raid if you can't get anywhere from much, much better to exponentially better gear than non-raiders.

The question itself seems to be a cross between sad and an outright admission of how the game has lost its course.

Perhaps you could just do it because you like to raid? There's a thought.

Doing something because you want to. In a game. For, like -- fun.

The one thing I hear most often from raiders is that they on the one hand want to be raiders and on the other hand have to be bribed to the skies to do it. And these are the guys who supposedly LIKE raiding!

It's a game, after all. If you don't want to raid, don't do it. Raiding, like other styles of play, should have something of its own reward in it. If it's no fun unless the rewards are enormous compared to any other style of gameplay, then it's not really that fun after all, is it? By design and by your own admission?

If it IS fun by design, then the ramping up in gear you get by raiding doesn't have to be so extreme to convince people to raid -- that is, to do something so many of raiding's most hard core defenders sound like they're claiming they can't understand the point of doing.

Raiders I think should have great gear, and maybe a bit better than the hardest working non-raiders. But when you get a 3600 point shaman and a 7000 point shaman, a 7k warrior and a 12k warrior, things have gone a little awry.

It's the "more is better" philosophy that EQ has had toward expansions and itemization and monsters. Upgrades used to be much more incremental, but now they are enormous. Very flashy. Elemental gear having 100hp per piece more than ornate AND having better focuses to boot is a clear example.

You need better gear to fight the mobs that drop elemental armor? Well, you at first killed those mobs without it, so that's not quite true. But, you would like upgrades, right? That make the zones/mobs easier to handle, so you can progress.

Fine. But if you don't design the armor to be so titanically superior, you don't have to make the monsters nearly as hard to kill to keep them from becoming trivial, and vice-versa. You tame a vicious cycle instead of exaggerating one in ways that will become even more exaggerated in every further expansion. I remember being quite happy with much smaller upgrades in HoT in Velious; everybody was. And I think a lot of people would agree that the game was better back then.

Once you go for the flash by making armor upgrades hugely better, you may get everyone excited and get people to buy your expansion, but you're going down a slow road to ruin for the game. You necessarily separate people out by their gear so one guy is not just better equipped than the other guy, but can quickly get better equipped to such an extent that you're tearing your customer base in half, and either have to design twice as much content or leave one or the other part of your customer base hanging, perhaps forever. Leaving you to lose a lot of customers either sooner or later, take your pick.

There should be armor upgrades, but human psychology and the way games work too is merely that we need rewards, not to hit the lottery. Smaller rewards are fine, and well in keeping with the spirit of the game and of challenge, that is -- you accomplish more, you get more. Not, you become a raider or you join a guild and within a few weeks you have 1500 more hitpoints and maybe a weapon that makes a critical difference. (BoW, anyone?)

Evening out the power curve in armor and the bosses and yard trash you kill to get it would not only make the game more coherent and sustainable, it would be to the great benefit of raiders too.

It would keep them from saying "Why raid?" Because the obvious answer would be, "What do you mean, why? Why do you like chocolate ice cream? I raid because I like raiding. It's fun!"

Instead of an answer that speaks more of being bribed and obedient than of doing something you like doing: "Because if I want to progress, I HAVE TO."

At this point, it would take Sony really rethinking their game and also spending a good amount of development time on it to let non-raiders have more avenues to catch up, since you don't want to nerf the raiders. That all sounds like really paying attention and even worse, spending money, one of which EQ developers has not shown a knack for and the other being completely off the table if you really have no vision for the game's present sustainability or how it's going to hold up in the potentially much broader marketplace of the incredibly near future -- as per Sony's track record.

Predicus
Ok, going to try to put some things into perspective.

To Iz:

1. Diablo 2

Your numbers on D2 are a bit skewed, and here is why. Blizzard has a strong player base in Korea and parts of Asia (very strong). I lived in Korea for three years, and from what I saw the population played online computer games to a greater level than your common American- and my guess European/Australian community. Korea's obsession with Blizzard and similar games that they dedicate television channels to gaming strategies and tournaments (not shows- channels). This is also a country with limited/non-existent copyright laws. For every purchased copy of D2/Warcraft/Starcraft there is countless hundreds of pirated copies. The key codes are pirated as well, so that the only people that are making a profit is the piraters selling for much lower than the going rate of the game purchase. While you may see 75k people online- my guess is about 1/3-1/2 of those players are playing pirated copies.
Blizzard also makes what I call "burnout games". Games are fast-paced and progression is limited compared to other competitors. (EQ)
The games, by themselves, burn out quickly, but provide a great diversion to players of long-term games when they are "burned-out". Many players who were avid EQ players will bounce back and forth between EQ and Blizzard to "avoid total burn-out", and that is where the "re-playability" comes into play.

2. GoD casual/raider

GoD sucks for progression....for the raider. The gear lines up through Uqua are nice, but the risk is also tremendously high. Many raid level mobs have lousy loot tables on top of this (1-4 class specific drops), and will commonly drop with placeholder loot (lots of berserker tomes dropping in GoD.) For many guilds, especially with so many retirees, PoTime generates faster upgrades. So, in general, GoD content is horrible for the PoTime raider. For the casual, GoD can be quite the opposite. Many of the trials have common drops that are Elemental/lower Time quality, and there are enough items to where a casual can completely re-outfit himself with just GoD loot. On top of this, trials leading up to Ikkinz revolve around the tanks hitpoints vs. healing power. DPS for the most part is the same in all the trials (charmed pet), and is therefore a constant for the casual/raider. As long as a group can keep the tank alive and the chanter pet under control, then the rewards are equal for the raider/casual. The rewards are bigger for the casual, though, in that they receive a large jump in stats/focus effects, where the raider may pass on most of the loot because they have slightly better. Therefore, the risk vs. reward is higher for the casual than for the raider. The current group I run with has 1 member that is guilded, with the rest of us running with lower raid (BoT, Velious)/LDoN/ornate gear, and we are very successful.

To Blargx:

1. Raid mentality:

Raids are only interesting until they are beaten to the point of triviality- then they become business as usual. While I am sure many raiders do truly enjoy raiding- many get just as burned out as the casual "grouping in LDoN". Raiding also is faster where the goal is equipment than Grouping where the goal is xp, hence burnout is quicker.
The concept is identical between the casual quitting over "not progressing" and the raider quitting over "not progressing". If equipment lines are equal between grouping/raiding the grouping does have the advantage with giving upgrades/xp than raiding that would give equipment. This leads into my second argument.

2. Gear Progression:

First, we have to take into account that SOE will purposely bottleneck content so that there is enough things to do that it will take enough time for them to develop and release new content. This is from a business standpoint where a producer wants to make sure that he can continue to generate money from a consuming society. The easiest way to achieve this without massive beta-testing on class balance is with gear. Now I am going to give an example of minimal gear progression:

Verant/SOE has released the following "high-end" expansions:

Original
Kunark
Velious
Luclin
PoP
GoD

Now lets say they went back and scaled the hitpoint/mob dps back some to where mobs gains 50 damage per hit per expansion and gear gains 10 hitpoints per piece for end-game content. We will say that the avg high end player has 1000 hitpoints at the beginning of the first expansions game.

Mob hit Total PC hitpoint
Original 100 1000
Kunark 150 1200 (20 gear slots) 20x10=200
Velious 200 1400
Luclin 250 1600
PoP 300 1800
GoD 350 2000

What you may begin to notice is that Mob dps and a player's gear are running off of different ratios. Where the original content may have a mob that can quad for 100's for a total dmg of 400- you see at GoD a mob could theoretically quad for 350's with a total dmg of 1400.

Mob vs PC (original) 400/1000= 2/5
Mob vs PC (GoD) 1400/2000=7/10

The answer would be to lower the mob's dps to preserve the "slow gear upgrade progress", and here is where we run into two conflicting problems: healer/dps raid ratio, and content bottlenecking. The developers need to keep the raiders occupied.

Healing: Lowering mob dps further doesn't solve the issue and here is why. If it takes 8 healers to keep a 1000 tank up vs. (original mob) then it is very likely that it may take only 10-12 healers to keep a tank up vs. (Kunark) mob because mob dps is only scales slightly from expansion to expansion. This will create the second problem.

Bottlenecking: To be consumer friendly when "bottlenecking" content the easiest answer is gear farming because it, in itself, is progression. We already know that long quests/rare drops/rare spawns make the consumers annoyed. A high-end guild's ultimate goal is to "farm" the high-end content, so if they can zip by "middle-content" with 10-12 healers and save 2-3 months then it is very likely that they will.

Of course, this is a simplified breakdown of gear vs. mob. You will also have to factor in raid dps vs. healing, raid make-up, and raid target scripts. Many of those, though, can be modified to work to the raider's advantage with a preference for healers/ heavy dps classes/ cc'ers. (Sound familiar? Check high-end guild class preferences)

SOE's current solution is higher hitpoints per piece of gear to create the "bottleneck" that we see in today's raid content.

(Ex: How many Vex Thal guilds actively farmed lower PoP in their progression to Elementals? Very few as much of the drops were near in stats to what they were currently equipped with.)

That's just bad design. It has doubtlessly cost Sony an enormous number of lost accounts, which is a very big mistake, because all money is good money. When you have an audience with no alternatives, you can be a lousy businessman. When alternatives start knocking at your door and are possibly soon to be stealing your lunch, a poor business plan can suddenly become much more apparent. It's okay to be thick-headed bumblers in a world that never changes. The problem is, the world does.

You spoke a lot about bottlenecking, Predicus. Bottlenecking is what you can do when you cater to very few types of players. It's what makes a game eventually turn into a niche game for a niche market.

Sony had the world in the palm of their hands for many, many years. If EQ's world were fully developed and lively, it could have been literally an experience that people passed on to their kids and paid decades worth of money for. It could have been worth untold billions to the company.

But you can't bottom-line that kind of thing and get that kind of reward. You have to spend money to make money, as they say. Fleshing out the world would have let people of every type and every level of ability and intelligence to commit time play around in the world forever and a day, or even come back to it years later. They forgot that what they're peddling to the public is an immersive world and a chatbox with graphics -- a social experiment in an enjoyable environment, not a min-max raid-or-die get the money and get the heck outta here as fast as possible before the whole thing comes crashing down on us diversion.

They truly had the ball as completely in their court as anyone ever could. And they decided that instead of giving everyone real content, they would cheap out on the development costs and just create bottlenecks. You don't like bottlenecks? Get lost. They decided that the game would turn more and more narrow until now it is about as narrow as a game like this conceivably could be. Almost anyone could write you out a chart in a minute saying EXACTLY how you're supposed to play -- exactly the zones you visit and how long you spend in them, exactly the tactics you use, exactly when you get your AA, which ones and where at what level, what gear you get, and how you get into raiding as quickly as possible and rocket through EQ quicker than a vaselined bar of Exlax through Richard Simmons' colon. The game has gone from being an open world with enormous potential to a rigid paint-by-numbers schematic for one type of customer and one type only, and the rest be damned.

They cheaped themselves out for the quick buck like you might with anything you have no confidence in and that you think has a very limited future. In not understanding their own product, they have lost countless millions of subscription dollars as people who the game wasn't designed around(read -- everyone but raiders) drifted away day by day, year by year. And they have, to use I think Blazz's phrase, painted themselves into a corner with their cheapness, where they can now expect not increasing profits but declining profits by virtue of years of their own bungling, as EQ slowly contracts and becomes the kind of game you get when you don't bother really fully committing resources to your business -- a niche game for cash that eventually becomes more and more trivial.

Some people have commented that EQ is now in the stage of the business lifecycle of being "milked," not grown. That's true if you assume it's a product with a very limited lifespan that can't really grow and won't benefit by growing anyway. But, as great as EQ can be as a game, it was from the beginning much more, and had vastly more potential.

It was Sony's singular short-sightedness and lack of vision that let it never come anywhere near that potential and closed out its incredibly open-ended future into relative irrelevancy.

Way to make a quick buck, guys.

That's what happens when you have the suits designing games instead of gamers.
Predicus
Some very good points, but going to point some items out.

Blizzard makes burn-out games?

Both Warcraft and Starcraft have years long enormous popularity.

There is one major difference between EQ and Starcraft/Warcraft, and that is EQ is PvE and Starcraft/Warcraft PvP. The PvE content of Blizzard's games burn-out way early, but because of their PvP element they have a strong staying power. PvP will always be superior "strategy-wise" than any programmed computer AI because their is an actual human being that you are reacting to. Game Developers only have to create a relatively balanced game, and then let the PvP'ers run amuck to keep a game going. The players are doing the work and in a sense "creating" the content.

Aside from that, I think we are seeing an evolution of the "middle class" of EQ. There are a few factors that went into this concept:

1. Game reaching the 5th year mark (aka: lots of un-used content)

2. Players generally disgruntled with 72 man raids.

3. LDoN/GoD content and EBay creating a stronger awareness on individual and other players skills.

4. EQ's aging community combined with less available time to play.

All of this combined is creating what I call the "casual raider". Guilds seem to be moving more towards a "skill/personality" form of recruitment, and are moving towards a "quality over quantity" approach to raid tactics. Even the non-guilded seem to be moving towards this concept. Pick-up raids are stronger than ever on most servers, and instead of 50 mindless players wiping over an event- we are seeing more knowledgable raid leaders and stronger group tactics. Older content while "trivial" in gear rewards to today's uber items are still worthy as upgrades, and are being hit by smaller groups. On Brell, there are pick-up raiders already farming the Elementals. There are also semi-open raids on Velious/Luclin content ran by groups of friends as opposed to zerg guilds.
This system seems to be working for Brell. Top-end content is left open for the large guilds with longer playtimes, and the lower content is eaten up by smaller guilds/raiders/friends. The casual raider still controls how much raiding vs grouping that they want- some raid 3-4 days a week, and others maybe once a week. The smaller numbers lead to better distribution of gear, and a better sense of accomplishment for the event that was completed. As more expansions come out, I expect that this "middle class" will become stronger. The individual players have always been good at evolving with good/bad content, and while I don't think SOE had this in mind- the players are finding a solution to the problem.

Beeg
The main complaint I see from non-raiders isnt the fact that there is SOME content that they cannot access, its the fact that with each expansion, there tends to be more and more "Raiders only" designed zones, thereby limiting what the casual player has access to. Original EQ had the 2 Dragons, and the 2 Planes, Fear and Hate.

Then Kunark had a bunch of dragons, but only 1 "real" pure-raid Zone, VP.

Velious added some more "Raid Only" areas, ST, ToV, even Kael to some degree. However, Velious also gave the game a TON of single group content, and wonderful progressive quests like Shawl, Coldain Ring, etc, where people could work towards a goal, and even if they didnt have a raid for that 8th Ring War, they had a dang nice item in the 7th ring.

Luclin is where it began to get REALLY seperated. Massive timesinks for keying into the "raid zones". Ssra and VT specifically designed for Raids. Single group content/quests in Luclin basically blew frogs, for the most part. Oh, and Luclin gave us the mother of all PL zones, PC. But still, 90% of the content WAS accessible by everyone.

PoP changed all that. MOST of the content was inaccessible, and even now, 2 years later, a TON of it STILL is. Even if you went on some pick-ups and got some flags, you were still likely shut out of the HUGE XP and outstanding 1-group loot in the EPs. Lets ignore TIme. I think people could accept Time if the EPs were more accessible. After all, Time is just sorta the "VP, NTOV, VT" of PoP.

Honestly, I think if the gap were a bit closer, there would be no bitching about the disparity. But its so huge. Just between the basic PoP stuff (ornate) and the next level, EP, there is a 3x jump in power. Thats too much, it really is. Especially with the MUDflated numbers we have in the game now, that the game NEEDS because of its age and progression. Heck, I think if they had just made the items a BIT closer, lets say: 50 hp/mana a slot for NToV, 75 for Ornate, 100 for VT, 130 for EP, 160 for Time, I doubt we would see as much of a stink being raised.

They screwed up. There are a lot of things they could do to help out the casual players, not tricky difficult things either. Fabled mobs were great. Why remove them? Revamping old zones is another great idea, but not if they dont itemize them with things the casual player wants, and not if the zones they pick are a total PITA (Droga, Chardok B, totally unacceptable casual zones, IMHO).

I dunno. Everyone has good points. There is no right or wrong. But the bottom line remains the same. There is a way to get the top loot. The game is designed that way. Play it that way, or don't.

Sudedor
Everyone seems to want to boil everything down to casual players vs raiding players. That isn't the bottom line. Never has been.

The bottom line is that *everyone* wants to be challenged.

Sure, EQ/Sony/Verant et al have created a monster by making the rift between casual players and raiding players immense. But, casual and raid are simply putting names on it.

Lets say a "casual" player levels himself to 65. Has "most" of his spells, and a mix of LDoN and bazaar equipment. Does that player want to continue to do LDoN normals? No, those are no longer challenging.

But where does he go?

You see, there is the problem. There is no content for a post 65 casual player to be challenged without being overwhelmed. It is either "raid" content, which requires a large number of players to simply handle the *(&# coming down, or trivial.

The problem is simply that there is no content between the two extremes.

I wish I knew the answer, but I don't.

Snoww Brokenhammer Retired Bashy bear E marr.

I have read most of the thread. I'd love to debate this idea with you, but I don't have time right now.

I agree with you on points and disagree on others. I'll give you a sort of bullet list of my opinions (mine, mind you, not always company policy)

No, people should not be able to attain the same gear through easier paths (like it or not, group content is easier than raid content, you've given many reasons why it is easier when you state why you don't like it).

Yes, there should be content for people to do that isn't raiding. Luckily, we have a lot of that.

If you don't like raiding but you do enjoy playing the game, then there is a lot of content to do with single groups. If you've actually done all of that consider a new character. I've enjoyed starting several characters, different viewpoints are nice.

Some folks have said things like - people should be able to get similar gear (just make the graphics nicer for the raid people) without raiding. I disagree in principle, as I've said before. I also disagree that this would satisfy those people. The day after such content was added they would be wondering why they don't also get the cool graphics.

Any game system with progression will have an amount of have's and have-nots. At just about any time in your progression you will be looking at people that have things that you can not attain at that time. That changes as you progress and as the game matures. Note that now players can take major Kunark dragons with a group. That wasn't so when that expansion was put out.

Stuff that was "raid only" becomes "two groups" and then becomes "single group" content. That's what happens when you have progression.

I'm just not going to debate the "casual" vs. "uber" thing here. I do want to repeat that if you are a casual player and you spend so much of your time yearning for items or abilities that are out of your reach that you don't enjoy playing, then you're not a casual player. A casual player plays in their free time to have some fun.

Snoww, you have to understand that we do hear you. We hear a lot of opinions, we read a lot of threads. It's just not possible to build one game that everyone can play the way they want to. We'd need a designer, a coder and an artist for every player. We do our best to make a game that people like to play. We do appreciate your feedback and we do read it. But at some point we have to make decision about how things will work. We want to make you happy just as much as we want to make everyone else happy.

I have no snappy ending for this. I certainly wrote more than I had planned - so I guess I'm staying late to get some work done :). I just wanted you to know that we do understand your point. On the team we'll have as many differing opinions about your ideas as were posted in this thread.

A
